>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>\r\nOpenRockets Blog\r\n\r\nSearch\r\n\r\nOpenRockets Magazine v3, Accounts.openrockets.com, 
Integrations, Corrections, Many more\r\nJune 13, 2026\r\n \r\n\r\nOpen Rockets Magazine â€” Structural Changes & 
Product Roadmap\r\nIt took hours to write this, and then AI rephrased. It took much longer than I thought, and about 4 
days. So please don't take this lightly. Read everything. \r\n1. The Problem with the Current Magazine\r\nThe magazine 
is currently doing terrible. Here is the first issue: magazine articles can only be created by accounts that are 
provided with a manually issued email and password â€” the email and password have to be created by us. Users cannot 
create their own. This is a manual process, and I understand that manual processes are sometimes necessary, but not 
here. We are building a complete ecosystem and we need to integrate everything properly.\r\n\r\n2. Build Order: What 
Comes First\r\n2.1 accounts.openrockets.com â€” The Unified Platform\r\nThe very first thing we need to build is 
accounts.openrockets.com. This is the unified platform for every single product we have â€” just like 
accounts.google.com for Google. Every product, including Zero Profit and the magazine, requires sign-in through 
accounts.openrockets.com. That is the first thing to build. Then Zero Profit. Then everything else around 
it.\r\n\r\nThe accounts.openrockets.com system needs a robust and clear API so we can:\r\n\r\nAllow users to sign up 
or log in\r\nRetrieve user data across all products\r\nHandle parental consent\r\n2.2 Zero Profit API\r\nAfter that, 
the next thing we need to build is the Zero Profit API. The Zero Profit core product is extremely valuable because 
everything else is going to be built around it. Zero Profit already has something called the Impact Factor Score â€” 
every single nonprofit in the Zero Profit ecosystem has one. Once Zero Profit is in place, we can use the Impact 
Factor Score to automatically grant nonprofits access to the magazine. This should not be done by invitation â€” it 
should be fully automated through the Zero Profit API.\r\n\r\n3. The Parental Consent Pipeline 
(accounts.openrockets.com)\r\nThis is a key part of the system. We cannot use services like Persona or Yoti for this. 
Both are extremely expensive because they use AI to process user data â€” verifying whether a person is human, 
determining if they are a minor or an adult, and checking IDs. That level of resource consumption is something we 
cannot handle right now. Even on Cloudflare, automated tools can cause API requests to peak at around 700,000 calls 
â€” we simply cannot possess that amount of resources at this stage. In the future, maybe. But not 
now.\r\n\r\nInstead, we are going to build our own solution. I am working on something called Kidentity 
(kidentity.org) â€” that is K + identity. I am currently running computer vision and pattern recognition work, and I 
will explain the full technical implementation in a separate blog post. Here is how the pipeline works:\r\n\r\nStep 1: 
Camera Access & Consent\r\nAfter the user gives their consent, we access the camera. We need separate terms and 
conditions specifically for this step. The system then uses age estimation to determine whether the person is a minor 
or an adult. The user should not have to manually select \"I am a minor\" or \"I am an adult\" â€” that can be easily 
spoofed by anyone. We detect it automatically.\r\n\r\nStep 2A: If an Adult is Detected\r\nRegister the adult.\r\nShow 
the consent forms. These cannot be generic forms â€” not Google Forms or anything like that, because those will not 
work here. We need API integration. We have to build a proper in-page consent form integrated directly into the 
platform. The form needs to include age certification and a signature widget. JavaScript libraries already exist for 
this â€” it is not a big deal to implement. Biometric fingerprint capture is also possible, but a signature is enough 
for now. We do not even need a signature if it turns out to be too complex â€” it depends.\r\nAfter the consent form 
is completed, the adult is registered and we move forward.\r\nStep 2B: If a Child is Detected\r\nRegister the child. 
The child is our customer and needs to be treated as such.\r\nPrompt for the parent. Tell the child: \"Getting 
parent.\"\r\nGet the parent in front of the camera.\r\nVerify the parent is an adult using the same face detection 
process.\r\nIf the parent is a first-time face to the camera, we register them and then show the parent the consent 
forms. Get the signature.\r\nAfter the parent's consent is captured, treat the minor as a normal user from that point 
forward.\r\nNote on ID verification: We are not requiring ID card verification right now. It would create too much 
friction â€” most kids will be able to bring a parent and get their face registered, but asking the parent to show an 
ID card is beyond what they would trust us with at this stage. Until we are covered in national newspapers, we verify 
by face only. Legally, at this point, we have sufficient information given to us by the user's own consent, which 
gives us the privilege to use their data going forward.\r\n\r\nStep 3: Collecting User Data\r\nAfter consent is 
obtained, collect the necessary data:\r\n\r\nMandatory: email address, parent's email address (already collected 
during the parental consent step via the integrated form)\r\nOptional: phone number\r\nAdditional: school, age, and 
other relevant fields\r\nFor minors, we do not collect signatures â€” just the necessary data.\r\n\r\nAfter collecting 
this data, create the account immediately. No further friction, no additional steps. The user has already invested 
their time into this process. If we pull something unnecessary at this point, it will be a horrible experience. Right 
after account creation, redirect them back to wherever they came from.\r\n\r\nStep 4: Redirecting the User Back\r\nUse 
two redundant methods to track where the user came from:\r\n\r\nURL parameter â€” track the origin in the URL\r\nLocal 
storage or session storage â€” store the origin as a fallback\r\nThis is critical because users may accidentally edit 
the URL, experience a redirect issue, or have a browser problem during the process. If we only rely on the URL 
parameter and the user edits it manually, they will be completely lost and confused about where they came from. They 
will have to log in again from the magazine and it will be a whole mess.\r\n\r\nOnce the account is created, check 
both sources and redirect them back â€” whether that is the magazine or another product. If both methods fail (for 
example, the browser blocks local storage or cookies), redirect to openrockets.com as a fallback.\r\n\r\nSince we are 
primarily using subdomains, the local storage data can be shared across all those domains without any issue. The only 
hurdle will be integrating this with Zero Profit, since it uses a separate domain.\r\n\r\nEmail Verification & 
Security\r\nEvery step needs proper email handling:\r\n\r\nEmail confirmation emails\r\nCertification emails\r\nForgot 
password emails\r\nWe need accurate, verified emails â€” especially since we are collecting parental email addresses 
as manual entries. There is a real risk of a child entering the wrong parent email, so email confirmation is mandatory 
at every point where an email is entered.\r\n\r\nThe reason we cannot simplify this (for example, just asking the 
child to type the parent's email and having the parent verify from their phone) is simple: a child can enter literally 
any email address in that box. That will not work and it will become a whole mess. The parental consent process 
described above is exactly how it needs to be done.\r\n\r\n4. Magazine â€” Open Access & Moderation\r\nWho Can 
Publish\r\nThe magazine should not be restricted to organizations only. Anyone with an accounts.openrockets.com 
account â€” which is free for everyone â€” should be able to create magazine articles. That is a great 
thing.\r\n\r\nReal names should be shown everywhere. Since we are using the same Open Rockets account across all 
products, there will be no pseudonyms and no online handles. Real names, real people. That is extremely valuable for 
authenticity â€” and it is important for journalists who may access the magazine.\r\n\r\nHandling Organization vs. 
Personal Accounts\r\nPersonal Open Rockets accounts are just that â€” personal. We should not split the account system 
into personal and organizational sign-ups. That is not going to work. Organizations are handled through Zero Profit. 
The magazine uses the same unified account for everyone.\r\n\r\nModeration\r\nThe magazine must have moderation. 
Without moderation it is going to be a whole mess. Here is how the moderation system should work:\r\n\r\nCreate a 
unified moderation portal at admin.openrockets.com. This is not connected to the normal accounts.openrockets.com 
system. It uses a separate, manually issued email and password â€” and maybe a separate security password on top of 
that. This portal moderates everything in one place:\r\n\r\nMagazine articles\r\nPress 
articles\r\nAdvertisements\r\nAny other content type\r\nEach product gets its own section inside 
admin.openrockets.com. So there will be a \"Magazine\" section, a \"Press\" section, an \"Advertisements\" section, 
and so on.\r\n\r\nThe moderation process is simple: look at the content, click accept or decline. That is it. Even if 
we moderate once per day or once per week, it will cost about five minutes. Just look at the content and hit accept or 
decline â€” it is a no-brainer.\r\n\r\nThe things to check for are:\r\n\r\nExplicit or offensive content\r\nHarmful 
content\r\nCopyright or IP violations\r\nCreative Commons license violations\r\nPersonally identifiable photos of 
unauthorized minors\r\nOffensive visual signs or hand gestures\r\nCensored or highly offensive imagery\r\nThis is also 
why AI cannot be used for moderation here. Unlike something like Google Drive, we have to simultaneously consider 
copyright, explicit content, offensive imagery, PII, and more. A manual reviewer handles all of this at 
once.\r\n\r\nThe benefit of one unified portal instead of separate moderation systems per product is clear: if one 
thing breaks, we fix it in one place. If we had separate moderation systems for press, magazine, and advertisements, 
the people moderating would have to log into three separate domains and monitor three separate systems. If one system 
breaks, we have to go into that system, find the issue, and fix it there. One portal, one fix. That is the right 
way.\r\n\r\nAlternative options: We could also integrate the moderation into a specific channel or department of 
club.openrockets.com â€” which is what I would suggest renaming the Open Rockets HR system to, since it sounds 
friendlier. Or we could integrate with the Discord API. Either way, the core idea is one centralized place.\r\n\r\n5. 
Magazine â€” Features & UI\r\nArticle Editing UI\r\nThe current magazine editing capabilities are not bad at all â€” 
they are good. But we need to scale this to hundreds, then thousands of users. That changes what the UI needs to be. 
The editing section needs to be very, very good.\r\n\r\nThe UI should look like a social media site. Here is what 
needs to change:\r\n\r\nMobile-friendly â€” the current UI is desktop-only. It is not mobile-friendly at all. This 
needs to be fixed.\r\nImage uploads â€” users should not have to paste image URLs. People will want to upload images 
they have created in Photoshop or elsewhere. There needs to be an upload button, with a clearly indicated maximum file 
size limit.\r\nFont â€” I believe the current font is Playfair Display (a serif font). It is fine. But explore adding 
more font options for the editor.\r\nFor design reference, look at Teen Vogue and TeenMagazine.com. These are the 
kinds of layouts and category differentiation we should be aiming for. The magazine should be as unified as possible 
while clearly differentiating between categories â€” that is valuable for the reader.\r\n\r\nStorage\r\n2TB of storage 
is sufficient for now. It will not be enough long-term once we scale to thousands of nonprofits, but it works for the 
current stage. We are not using Amazon AWS â€” it is too expensive. Cloudflare is an option, or other safe alternative 
providers.\r\n\r\nMetrics & Engagement\r\nCurrently, the only metric shown is view count. That is not enough. We need 
a proper metrics dashboard in the user profile. Here is what to add:\r\n\r\nComments section per article â€” this is 
also valuable for SEO\r\nHearts/likes â€” use a heart icon, because that is what teenagers are familiar with from 
Instagram, Discord, and similar platforms\r\nDislikes â€” use a thumbs down or a broken heart icon\r\nMore detailed 
metrics in the user dashboard\r\nHumanize this as much as possible. These are the things that will actually make 
people want to use it and come back.\r\n\r\nSchema Markup, robots.txt & Link Previews\r\nSchema is the baseline for 
everything SEO-related. It is just an HTML tag â€” not a big deal to implement, but it is extremely valuable. In 
addition to that:\r\n\r\nAdd a robots.txt file for the magazine folder\r\nGenerate schema markup automatically for 
each article at the moment it is published\r\nGenerate link preview metadata (Open Graph / meta tags) for every 
article â€” so when someone shares an article link, the preview shows the correct title, image, and description. 
Before someone clicks a link, they need to understand what they are clicking. This is very important and it will drive 
more traffic.\r\n6. Distribution & SEO â€” Triggered on Moderation Approval\r\nThis is one of the most important 
things. Right after a moderator clicks \"accept\" on a magazine article in admin.openrockets.com, it should trigger a 
Cloudflare Worker that automatically does all of the following:\r\n\r\nPing Microsoft Bing â€” submit the article URL 
for indexing\r\nPing-O-Matic â€” ping the aggregator (visit the site, get the API, integrate it)\r\nPost to Bluesky 
â€” Bluesky offers a completely free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Bluesky profile for 
this.\r\nPost to Mastodon â€” Mastodon also offers a free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Mastodon 
profile.\r\nSubmit to Hacker News (Y Combinator) â€” Hacker News is an exceptionally high-value aggregator. A good 
post there can get hundreds of views on its own.\r\nLook for any additional free API providers where we can ping or 
submit content for indexing â€” there are more out there.\r\n\r\nTwitter/X: Getting the Twitter API requires 
purchasing X Premium, which is not worth the cost right now. Skip it for now and use the free API providers 
instead.\r\n\r\nThe trigger point is the moderation approval â€” not the article submission. The flow is: article 
submitted â†’ goes to admin panel â†’ moderated â†’ if accepted, Cloudflare Worker fires and distributes it 
everywhere. That is the correct sequence and it is why thinking through all of this in advance is so 
valuable.\r\n\r\n7. Magazine Credibility Strategy\r\nYou might think: if the magazine is open to every single person, 
it will not have any value or credibility. Here is the strategy:\r\n\r\nRight now, open it to everyone.\r\nDo not 
market it as an open platform. Never say \"anyone can publish here.\"\r\nOnce the magazine gets a significant amount 
of traffic, start restricting new signups â€” minimize or reduce the intake of new publishers at that point.\r\nThis 
is how we build credibility over time without sacrificing early growth.\r\n\r\n8. Advertisement System\r\nThe 
advertisement system is for two separate entities: for-profit entities and nonprofit entities. Nonprofit entities will 
be the majority, but we will also be able to earn from for-profit advertisements. I will go into the full details of 
how the advertisement system works in a separate blog post â€” please read that one too, because it contains a 
significant amount of relevant information.\r\n\r\nFor moderation purposes, the advertisement section in 
admin.openrockets.com handles all of this. We do not need to think about whether an advertisement is from a for-profit 
or nonprofit entity when moderating. The only thing we need to check is the same as everything else: is it sensitive, 
personally identifiable, explicit, offensive, a copyright violation, and so on. Same process, same portal.\r\n\r\n9. 
Future Plans (Not Now â€” But Worth Noting)\r\nInstagram API integration â€” When someone publishes a magazine 
article, it could also be automatically posted to their connected Instagram account. Getting the Instagram API is a 
complicated process, so this is a future addition.\r\nMore social platform integrations â€” Same idea, expanded to 
other platforms.\r\n10. Final Note\r\nThe magazine is an extremely important credibility factor for Open Rockets. We 
have no future if we do not get into national newspapers. Every single thing described in this document is possible to 
implement â€” and it is the greatest product we could build. I have spent hours thinking through these ideas and all 
of them can be done.\r\n\r\nIf any changes are needed to the Open Rockets Magazine logo, never hesitate â€” make those 
changes. Just make sure they are reflected everywhere across all sites we 
have.\r\n\r\nShare\r\nShare\r\nComments\r\n\r\nPopular posts from this blog\r\nStarting a High School Nonprofit? Read

Blog\r\n\r\nSearch\r\n\r\nOpenRockets Magazine v3, Accounts.openrockets.com, Integrations, Corrections, Many 
more\r\nJune 13, 2026\r\n \r\n\r\nOpen Rockets Magazine â€” Structural Changes & Product Roadmap\r\nIt took hours to 
write this, and then AI rephrased. It took much longer than I thought, and about 4 days. So please don't take this 
lightly. Read everything. \r\n1. The Problem with the Current Magazine\r\nThe magazine is currently doing terrible. 
Here is the first issue: magazine articles can only be created by accounts that are provided with a manually issued 
email and password â€” the email and password have to be created by us. Users cannot create their own. This is a 
manual process, and I understand that manual processes are sometimes necessary, but not here. We are building a 
complete ecosystem and we need to integrate everything properly.\r\n\r\n2. Build Order: What Comes First\r\n2.1 
accounts.openrockets.com â€” The Unified Platform\r\nThe very first thing we need to build is 
accounts.openrockets.com. This is the unified platform for every single product we have â€” just like 
accounts.google.com for Google. Every product, including Zero Profit and the magazine, requires sign-in through 
accounts.openrockets.com. That is the first thing to build. Then Zero Profit. Then everything else around 
it.\r\n\r\nThe accounts.openrockets.com system needs a robust and clear API so we can:\r\n\r\nAllow users to sign up 
or log in\r\nRetrieve user data across all products\r\nHandle parental consent\r\n2.2 Zero Profit API\r\nAfter that, 
the next thing we need to build is the Zero Profit API. The Zero Profit core product is extremely valuable because 
everything else is going to be built around it. Zero Profit already has something called the Impact Factor Score â€” 
every single nonprofit in the Zero Profit ecosystem has one. Once Zero Profit is in place, we can use the Impact 
Factor Score to automatically grant nonprofits access to the magazine. This should not be done by invitation â€” it 
should be fully automated through the Zero Profit API.\r\n\r\n3. The Parental Consent Pipeline 
(accounts.openrockets.com)\r\nThis is a key part of the system. We cannot use services like Persona or Yoti for this. 
Both are extremely expensive because they use AI to process user data â€” verifying whether a person is human, 
determining if they are a minor or an adult, and checking IDs. That level of resource consumption is something we 
cannot handle right now. Even on Cloudflare, automated tools can cause API requests to peak at around 700,000 calls 
â€” we simply cannot possess that amount of resources at this stage. In the future, maybe. But not 
now.\r\n\r\nInstead, we are going to build our own solution. I am working on something called Kidentity 
(kidentity.org) â€” that is K + identity. I am currently running computer vision and pattern recognition work, and I 
will explain the full technical implementation in a separate blog post. Here is how the pipeline works:\r\n\r\nStep 1: 
Camera Access & Consent\r\nAfter the user gives their consent, we access the camera. We need separate terms and 
conditions specifically for this step. The system then uses age estimation to determine whether the person is a minor 
or an adult. The user should not have to manually select \"I am a minor\" or \"I am an adult\" â€” that can be easily 
spoofed by anyone. We detect it automatically.\r\n\r\nStep 2A: If an Adult is Detected\r\nRegister the adult.\r\nShow 
the consent forms. These cannot be generic forms â€” not Google Forms or anything like that, because those will not 
work here. We need API integration. We have to build a proper in-page consent form integrated directly into the 
platform. The form needs to include age certification and a signature widget. JavaScript libraries already exist for 
this â€” it is not a big deal to implement. Biometric fingerprint capture is also possible, but a signature is enough 
for now. We do not even need a signature if it turns out to be too complex â€” it depends.\r\nAfter the consent form 
is completed, the adult is registered and we move forward.\r\nStep 2B: If a Child is Detected\r\nRegister the child. 
The child is our customer and needs to be treated as such.\r\nPrompt for the parent. Tell the child: \"Getting 
parent.\"\r\nGet the parent in front of the camera.\r\nVerify the parent is an adult using the same face detection 
process.\r\nIf the parent is a first-time face to the camera, we register them and then show the parent the consent 
forms. Get the signature.\r\nAfter the parent's consent is captured, treat the minor as a normal user from that point 
forward.\r\nNote on ID verification: We are not requiring ID card verification right now. It would create too much 
friction â€” most kids will be able to bring a parent and get their face registered, but asking the parent to show an 
ID card is beyond what they would trust us with at this stage. Until we are covered in national newspapers, we verify 
by face only. Legally, at this point, we have sufficient information given to us by the user's own consent, which 
gives us the privilege to use their data going forward.\r\n\r\nStep 3: Collecting User Data\r\nAfter consent is 
obtained, collect the necessary data:\r\n\r\nMandatory: email address, parent's email address (already collected 
during the parental consent step via the integrated form)\r\nOptional: phone number\r\nAdditional: school, age, and 
other relevant fields\r\nFor minors, we do not collect signatures â€” just the necessary data.\r\n\r\nAfter collecting 
this data, create the account immediately. No further friction, no additional steps. The user has already invested 
their time into this process. If we pull something unnecessary at this point, it will be a horrible experience. Right 
after account creation, redirect them back to wherever they came from.\r\n\r\nStep 4: Redirecting the User Back\r\nUse 
two redundant methods to track where the user came from:\r\n\r\nURL parameter â€” track the origin in the URL\r\nLocal 
storage or session storage â€” store the origin as a fallback\r\nThis is critical because users may accidentally edit 
the URL, experience a redirect issue, or have a browser problem during the process. If we only rely on the URL 
parameter and the user edits it manually, they will be completely lost and confused about where they came from. They 
will have to log in again from the magazine and it will be a whole mess.\r\n\r\nOnce the account is created, check 
both sources and redirect them back â€” whether that is the magazine or another product. If both methods fail (for 
example, the browser blocks local storage or cookies), redirect to openrockets.com as a fallback.\r\n\r\nSince we are 
primarily using subdomains, the local storage data can be shared across all those domains without any issue. The only 
hurdle will be integrating this with Zero Profit, since it uses a separate domain.\r\n\r\nEmail Verification & 
Security\r\nEvery step needs proper email handling:\r\n\r\nEmail confirmation emails\r\nCertification emails\r\nForgot 
password emails\r\nWe need accurate, verified emails â€” especially since we are collecting parental email addresses 
as manual entries. There is a real risk of a child entering the wrong parent email, so email confirmation is mandatory 
at every point where an email is entered.\r\n\r\nThe reason we cannot simplify this (for example, just asking the 
child to type the parent's email and having the parent verify from their phone) is simple: a child can enter literally 
any email address in that box. That will not work and it will become a whole mess. The parental consent process 
described above is exactly how it needs to be done.\r\n\r\n4. Magazine â€” Open Access & Moderation\r\nWho Can 
Publish\r\nThe magazine should not be restricted to organizations only. Anyone with an accounts.openrockets.com 
account â€” which is free for everyone â€” should be able to create magazine articles. That is a great 
thing.\r\n\r\nReal names should be shown everywhere. Since we are using the same Open Rockets account across all 
products, there will be no pseudonyms and no online handles. Real names, real people. That is extremely valuable for 
authenticity â€” and it is important for journalists who may access the magazine.\r\n\r\nHandling Organization vs. 
Personal Accounts\r\nPersonal Open Rockets accounts are just that â€” personal. We should not split the account system 
into personal and organizational sign-ups. That is not going to work. Organizations are handled through Zero Profit. 
The magazine uses the same unified account for everyone.\r\n\r\nModeration\r\nThe magazine must have moderation. 
Without moderation it is going to be a whole mess. Here is how the moderation system should work:\r\n\r\nCreate a 
unified moderation portal at admin.openrockets.com. This is not connected to the normal accounts.openrockets.com 
system. It uses a separate, manually issued email and password â€” and maybe a separate security password on top of 
that. This portal moderates everything in one place:\r\n\r\nMagazine articles\r\nPress 
articles\r\nAdvertisements\r\nAny other content type\r\nEach product gets its own section inside 
admin.openrockets.com. So there will be a \"Magazine\" section, a \"Press\" section, an \"Advertisements\" section, 
and so on.\r\n\r\nThe moderation process is simple: look at the content, click accept or decline. That is it. Even if 
we moderate once per day or once per week, it will cost about five minutes. Just look at the content and hit accept or 
decline â€” it is a no-brainer.\r\n\r\nThe things to check for are:\r\n\r\nExplicit or offensive content\r\nHarmful 
content\r\nCopyright or IP violations\r\nCreative Commons license violations\r\nPersonally identifiable photos of 
unauthorized minors\r\nOffensive visual signs or hand gestures\r\nCensored or highly offensive imagery\r\nThis is also 
why AI cannot be used for moderation here. Unlike something like Google Drive, we have to simultaneously consider 
copyright, explicit content, offensive imagery, PII, and more. A manual reviewer handles all of this at 
once.\r\n\r\nThe benefit of one unified portal instead of separate moderation systems per product is clear: if one 
thing breaks, we fix it in one place. If we had separate moderation systems for press, magazine, and advertisements, 
the people moderating would have to log into three separate domains and monitor three separate systems. If one system 
breaks, we have to go into that system, find the issue, and fix it there. One portal, one fix. That is the right 
way.\r\n\r\nAlternative options: We could also integrate the moderation into a specific channel or department of 
club.openrockets.com â€” which is what I would suggest renaming the Open Rockets HR system to, since it sounds 
friendlier. Or we could integrate with the Discord API. Either way, the core idea is one centralized place.\r\n\r\n5. 
Magazine â€” Features & UI\r\nArticle Editing UI\r\nThe current magazine editing capabilities are not bad at all â€” 
they are good. But we need to scale this to hundreds, then thousands of users. That changes what the UI needs to be. 
The editing section needs to be very, very good.\r\n\r\nThe UI should look like a social media site. Here is what 
needs to change:\r\n\r\nMobile-friendly â€” the current UI is desktop-only. It is not mobile-friendly at all. This 
needs to be fixed.\r\nImage uploads â€” users should not have to paste image URLs. People will want to upload images 
they have created in Photoshop or elsewhere. There needs to be an upload button, with a clearly indicated maximum file 
size limit.\r\nFont â€” I believe the current font is Playfair Display (a serif font). It is fine. But explore adding 
more font options for the editor.\r\nFor design reference, look at Teen Vogue and TeenMagazine.com. These are the 
kinds of layouts and category differentiation we should be aiming for. The magazine should be as unified as possible 
while clearly differentiating between categories â€” that is valuable for the reader.\r\n\r\nStorage\r\n2TB of storage 
is sufficient for now. It will not be enough long-term once we scale to thousands of nonprofits, but it works for the 
current stage. We are not using Amazon AWS â€” it is too expensive. Cloudflare is an option, or other safe alternative 
providers.\r\n\r\nMetrics & Engagement\r\nCurrently, the only metric shown is view count. That is not enough. We need 
a proper metrics dashboard in the user profile. Here is what to add:\r\n\r\nComments section per article â€” this is 
also valuable for SEO\r\nHearts/likes â€” use a heart icon, because that is what teenagers are familiar with from 
Instagram, Discord, and similar platforms\r\nDislikes â€” use a thumbs down or a broken heart icon\r\nMore detailed 
metrics in the user dashboard\r\nHumanize this as much as possible. These are the things that will actually make 
people want to use it and come back.\r\n\r\nSchema Markup, robots.txt & Link Previews\r\nSchema is the baseline for 
everything SEO-related. It is just an HTML tag â€” not a big deal to implement, but it is extremely valuable. In 
addition to that:\r\n\r\nAdd a robots.txt file for the magazine folder\r\nGenerate schema markup automatically for 
each article at the moment it is published\r\nGenerate link preview metadata (Open Graph / meta tags) for every 
article â€” so when someone shares an article link, the preview shows the correct title, image, and description. 
Before someone clicks a link, they need to understand what they are clicking. This is very important and it will drive 
more traffic.\r\n6. Distribution & SEO â€” Triggered on Moderation Approval\r\nThis is one of the most important 
things. Right after a moderator clicks \"accept\" on a magazine article in admin.openrockets.com, it should trigger a 
Cloudflare Worker that automatically does all of the following:\r\n\r\nPing Microsoft Bing â€” submit the article URL 
for indexing\r\nPing-O-Matic â€” ping the aggregator (visit the site, get the API, integrate it)\r\nPost to Bluesky 
â€” Bluesky offers a completely free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Bluesky profile for 
this.\r\nPost to Mastodon â€” Mastodon also offers a free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Mastodon 
profile.\r\nSubmit to Hacker News (Y Combinator) â€” Hacker News is an exceptionally high-value aggregator. A good 
post there can get hundreds of views on its own.\r\nLook for any additional free API providers where we can ping or 
submit content for indexing â€” there are more out there.\r\n\r\nTwitter/X: Getting the Twitter API requires 
purchasing X Premium, which is not worth the cost right now. Skip it for now and use the free API providers 
instead.\r\n\r\nThe trigger point is the moderation approval â€” not the article submission. The flow is: article 
submitted â†’ goes to admin panel â†’ moderated â†’ if accepted, Cloudflare Worker fires and distributes it 
everywhere. That is the correct sequence and it is why thinking through all of this in advance is so 
valuable.\r\n\r\n7. Magazine Credibility Strategy\r\nYou might think: if the magazine is open to every single person, 
it will not have any value or credibility. Here is the strategy:\r\n\r\nRight now, open it to everyone.\r\nDo not 
market it as an open platform. Never say \"anyone can publish here.\"\r\nOnce the magazine gets a significant amount 
of traffic, start restricting new signups â€” minimize or reduce the intake of new publishers at that point.\r\nThis 
is how we build credibility over time without sacrificing early growth.\r\n\r\n8. Advertisement System\r\nThe 
advertisement system is for two separate entities: for-profit entities and nonprofit entities. Nonprofit entities will 
be the majority, but we will also be able to earn from for-profit advertisements. I will go into the full details of 
how the advertisement system works in a separate blog post â€” please read that one too, because it contains a 
significant amount of relevant information.\r\n\r\nFor moderation purposes, the advertisement section in 
admin.openrockets.com handles all of this. We do not need to think about whether an advertisement is from a for-profit 
or nonprofit entity when moderating. The only thing we need to check is the same as everything else: is it sensitive, 
personally identifiable, explicit, offensive, a copyright violation, and so on. Same process, same portal.\r\n\r\n9. 
Future Plans (Not Now â€” But Worth Noting)\r\nInstagram API integration â€” When someone publishes a magazine 
article, it could also be automatically posted to their connected Instagram account. Getting the Instagram API is a 
complicated process, so this is a future addition.\r\nMore social platform integrations â€” Same idea, expanded to 
other platforms.\r\n10. Final Note\r\nThe magazine is an extremely important credibility factor for Open Rockets. We 
have no future if we do not get into national newspapers. Every single thing described in this document is possible to 
implement â€” and it is the greatest product we could build. I have spent hours thinking through these ideas and all 
of them can be done.\r\n\r\nIf any changes are needed to the Open Rockets Magazine logo, never hesitate â€” make those 
changes. Just make sure they are reflected everywhere across all sites we 
have.\r\n\r\nShare\r\nShare\r\nComments\r\n\r\nPopular posts from this blog\r\nStarting a High School Nonprofit? Read

><>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>\r\nOpenRockets 
Blog\r\n\r\nSearch\r\n\r\nOpenRockets Magazine v3, Accounts.openrockets.com, Integrations, Corrections, Many 
more\r\nJune 13, 2026\r\n \r\n\r\nOpen Rockets Magazine â€” Structural Changes & Product Roadmap\r\nIt took hours to 
write this, and then AI rephrased. It took much longer than I thought, and about 4 days. So please don't take this 
lightly. Read everything. \r\n1. The Problem with the Current Magazine\r\nThe magazine is currently doing terrible. 
Here is the first issue: magazine articles can only be created by accounts that are provided with a manually issued 
email and password â€” the email and password have to be created by us. Users cannot create their own. This is a 
manual process, and I understand that manual processes are sometimes necessary, but not here. We are building a 
complete ecosystem and we need to integrate everything properly.\r\n\r\n2. Build Order: What Comes First\r\n2.1 
accounts.openrockets.com â€” The Unified Platform\r\nThe very first thing we need to build is 
accounts.openrockets.com. This is the unified platform for every single product we have â€” just like 
accounts.google.com for Google. Every product, including Zero Profit and the magazine, requires sign-in through 
accounts.openrockets.com. That is the first thing to build. Then Zero Profit. Then everything else around 
it.\r\n\r\nThe accounts.openrockets.com system needs a robust and clear API so we can:\r\n\r\nAllow users to sign up 
or log in\r\nRetrieve user data across all products\r\nHandle parental consent\r\n2.2 Zero Profit API\r\nAfter that, 
the next thing we need to build is the Zero Profit API. The Zero Profit core product is extremely valuable because 
everything else is going to be built around it. Zero Profit already has something called the Impact Factor Score â€” 
every single nonprofit in the Zero Profit ecosystem has one. Once Zero Profit is in place, we can use the Impact 
Factor Score to automatically grant nonprofits access to the magazine. This should not be done by invitation â€” it 
should be fully automated through the Zero Profit API.\r\n\r\n3. The Parental Consent Pipeline 
(accounts.openrockets.com)\r\nThis is a key part of the system. We cannot use services like Persona or Yoti for this. 
Both are extremely expensive because they use AI to process user data â€” verifying whether a person is human, 
determining if they are a minor or an adult, and checking IDs. That level of resource consumption is something we 
cannot handle right now. Even on Cloudflare, automated tools can cause API requests to peak at around 700,000 calls 
â€” we simply cannot possess that amount of resources at this stage. In the future, maybe. But not 
now.\r\n\r\nInstead, we are going to build our own solution. I am working on something called Kidentity 
(kidentity.org) â€” that is K + identity. I am currently running computer vision and pattern recognition work, and I 
will explain the full technical implementation in a separate blog post. Here is how the pipeline works:\r\n\r\nStep 1: 
Camera Access & Consent\r\nAfter the user gives their consent, we access the camera. We need separate terms and 
conditions specifically for this step. The system then uses age estimation to determine whether the person is a minor 
or an adult. The user should not have to manually select \"I am a minor\" or \"I am an adult\" â€” that can be easily 
spoofed by anyone. We detect it automatically.\r\n\r\nStep 2A: If an Adult is Detected\r\nRegister the adult.\r\nShow 
the consent forms. These cannot be generic forms â€” not Google Forms or anything like that, because those will not 
work here. We need API integration. We have to build a proper in-page consent form integrated directly into the 
platform. The form needs to include age certification and a signature widget. JavaScript libraries already exist for 
this â€” it is not a big deal to implement. Biometric fingerprint capture is also possible, but a signature is enough 
for now. We do not even need a signature if it turns out to be too complex â€” it depends.\r\nAfter the consent form 
is completed, the adult is registered and we move forward.\r\nStep 2B: If a Child is Detected\r\nRegister the child. 
The child is our customer and needs to be treated as such.\r\nPrompt for the parent. Tell the child: \"Getting 
parent.\"\r\nGet the parent in front of the camera.\r\nVerify the parent is an adult using the same face detection 
process.\r\nIf the parent is a first-time face to the camera, we register them and then show the parent the consent 
forms. Get the signature.\r\nAfter the parent's consent is captured, treat the minor as a normal user from that point 
forward.\r\nNote on ID verification: We are not requiring ID card verification right now. It would create too much 
friction â€” most kids will be able to bring a parent and get their face registered, but asking the parent to show an 
ID card is beyond what they would trust us with at this stage. Until we are covered in national newspapers, we verify 
by face only. Legally, at this point, we have sufficient information given to us by the user's own consent, which 
gives us the privilege to use their data going forward.\r\n\r\nStep 3: Collecting User Data\r\nAfter consent is 
obtained, collect the necessary data:\r\n\r\nMandatory: email address, parent's email address (already collected 
during the parental consent step via the integrated form)\r\nOptional: phone number\r\nAdditional: school, age, and 
other relevant fields\r\nFor minors, we do not collect signatures â€” just the necessary data.\r\n\r\nAfter collecting 
this data, create the account immediately. No further friction, no additional steps. The user has already invested 
their time into this process. If we pull something unnecessary at this point, it will be a horrible experience. Right 
after account creation, redirect them back to wherever they came from.\r\n\r\nStep 4: Redirecting the User Back\r\nUse 
two redundant methods to track where the user came from:\r\n\r\nURL parameter â€” track the origin in the URL\r\nLocal 
storage or session storage â€” store the origin as a fallback\r\nThis is critical because users may accidentally edit 
the URL, experience a redirect issue, or have a browser problem during the process. If we only rely on the URL 
parameter and the user edits it manually, they will be completely lost and confused about where they came from. They 
will have to log in again from the magazine and it will be a whole mess.\r\n\r\nOnce the account is created, check 
both sources and redirect them back â€” whether that is the magazine or another product. If both methods fail (for 
example, the browser blocks local storage or cookies), redirect to openrockets.com as a fallback.\r\n\r\nSince we are 
primarily using subdomains, the local storage data can be shared across all those domains without any issue. The only 
hurdle will be integrating this with Zero Profit, since it uses a separate domain.\r\n\r\nEmail Verification & 
Security\r\nEvery step needs proper email handling:\r\n\r\nEmail confirmation emails\r\nCertification emails\r\nForgot 
password emails\r\nWe need accurate, verified emails â€” especially since we are collecting parental email addresses 
as manual entries. There is a real risk of a child entering the wrong parent email, so email confirmation is mandatory 
at every point where an email is entered.\r\n\r\nThe reason we cannot simplify this (for example, just asking the 
child to type the parent's email and having the parent verify from their phone) is simple: a child can enter literally 
any email address in that box. That will not work and it will become a whole mess. The parental consent process 
described above is exactly how it needs to be done.\r\n\r\n4. Magazine â€” Open Access & Moderation\r\nWho Can 
Publish\r\nThe magazine should not be restricted to organizations only. Anyone with an accounts.openrockets.com 
account â€” which is free for everyone â€” should be able to create magazine articles. That is a great 
thing.\r\n\r\nReal names should be shown everywhere. Since we are using the same Open Rockets account across all 
products, there will be no pseudonyms and no online handles. Real names, real people. That is extremely valuable for 
authenticity â€” and it is important for journalists who may access the magazine.\r\n\r\nHandling Organization vs. 
Personal Accounts\r\nPersonal Open Rockets accounts are just that â€” personal. We should not split the account system 
into personal and organizational sign-ups. That is not going to work. Organizations are handled through Zero Profit. 
The magazine uses the same unified account for everyone.\r\n\r\nModeration\r\nThe magazine must have moderation. 
Without moderation it is going to be a whole mess. Here is how the moderation system should work:\r\n\r\nCreate a 
unified moderation portal at admin.openrockets.com. This is not connected to the normal accounts.openrockets.com 
system. It uses a separate, manually issued email and password â€” and maybe a separate security password on top of 
that. This portal moderates everything in one place:\r\n\r\nMagazine articles\r\nPress 
articles\r\nAdvertisements\r\nAny other content type\r\nEach product gets its own section inside 
admin.openrockets.com. So there will be a \"Magazine\" section, a \"Press\" section, an \"Advertisements\" section, 
and so on.\r\n\r\nThe moderation process is simple: look at the content, click accept or decline. That is it. Even if 
we moderate once per day or once per week, it will cost about five minutes. Just look at the content and hit accept or 
decline â€” it is a no-brainer.\r\n\r\nThe things to check for are:\r\n\r\nExplicit or offensive content\r\nHarmful 
content\r\nCopyright or IP violations\r\nCreative Commons license violations\r\nPersonally identifiable photos of 
unauthorized minors\r\nOffensive visual signs or hand gestures\r\nCensored or highly offensive imagery\r\nThis is also 
why AI cannot be used for moderation here. Unlike something like Google Drive, we have to simultaneously consider 
copyright, explicit content, offensive imagery, PII, and more. A manual reviewer handles all of this at 
once.\r\n\r\nThe benefit of one unified portal instead of separate moderation systems per product is clear: if one 
thing breaks, we fix it in one place. If we had separate moderation systems for press, magazine, and advertisements, 
the people moderating would have to log into three separate domains and monitor three separate systems. If one system 
breaks, we have to go into that system, find the issue, and fix it there. One portal, one fix. That is the right 
way.\r\n\r\nAlternative options: We could also integrate the moderation into a specific channel or department of 
club.openrockets.com â€” which is what I would suggest renaming the Open Rockets HR system to, since it sounds 
friendlier. Or we could integrate with the Discord API. Either way, the core idea is one centralized place.\r\n\r\n5. 
Magazine â€” Features & UI\r\nArticle Editing UI\r\nThe current magazine editing capabilities are not bad at all â€” 
they are good. But we need to scale this to hundreds, then thousands of users. That changes what the UI needs to be. 
The editing section needs to be very, very good.\r\n\r\nThe UI should look like a social media site. Here is what 
needs to change:\r\n\r\nMobile-friendly â€” the current UI is desktop-only. It is not mobile-friendly at all. This 
needs to be fixed.\r\nImage uploads â€” users should not have to paste image URLs. People will want to upload images 
they have created in Photoshop or elsewhere. There needs to be an upload button, with a clearly indicated maximum file 
size limit.\r\nFont â€” I believe the current font is Playfair Display (a serif font). It is fine. But explore adding 
more font options for the editor.\r\nFor design reference, look at Teen Vogue and TeenMagazine.com. These are the 
kinds of layouts and category differentiation we should be aiming for. The magazine should be as unified as possible 
while clearly differentiating between categories â€” that is valuable for the reader.\r\n\r\nStorage\r\n2TB of storage 
is sufficient for now. It will not be enough long-term once we scale to thousands of nonprofits, but it works for the 
current stage. We are not using Amazon AWS â€” it is too expensive. Cloudflare is an option, or other safe alternative 
providers.\r\n\r\nMetrics & Engagement\r\nCurrently, the only metric shown is view count. That is not enough. We need 
a proper metrics dashboard in the user profile. Here is what to add:\r\n\r\nComments section per article â€” this is 
also valuable for SEO\r\nHearts/likes â€” use a heart icon, because that is what teenagers are familiar with from 
Instagram, Discord, and similar platforms\r\nDislikes â€” use a thumbs down or a broken heart icon\r\nMore detailed 
metrics in the user dashboard\r\nHumanize this as much as possible. These are the things that will actually make 
people want to use it and come back.\r\n\r\nSchema Markup, robots.txt & Link Previews\r\nSchema is the baseline for 
everything SEO-related. It is just an HTML tag â€” not a big deal to implement, but it is extremely valuable. In 
addition to that:\r\n\r\nAdd a robots.txt file for the magazine folder\r\nGenerate schema markup automatically for 
each article at the moment it is published\r\nGenerate link preview metadata (Open Graph / meta tags) for every 
article â€” so when someone shares an article link, the preview shows the correct title, image, and description. 
Before someone clicks a link, they need to understand what they are clicking. This is very important and it will drive 
more traffic.\r\n6. Distribution & SEO â€” Triggered on Moderation Approval\r\nThis is one of the most important 
things. Right after a moderator clicks \"accept\" on a magazine article in admin.openrockets.com, it should trigger a 
Cloudflare Worker that automatically does all of the following:\r\n\r\nPing Microsoft Bing â€” submit the article URL 
for indexing\r\nPing-O-Matic â€” ping the aggregator (visit the site, get the API, integrate it)\r\nPost to Bluesky 
â€” Bluesky offers a completely free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Bluesky profile for 
this.\r\nPost to Mastodon â€” Mastodon also offers a free API. Create a separate \"Open Rockets Magazine\" Mastodon 
profile.\r\nSubmit to Hacker News (Y Combinator) â€” Hacker News is an exceptionally high-value aggregator. A good 
post there can get hundreds of views on its own.\r\nLook for any additional free API providers where we can ping or 
submit content for indexing â€” there are more out there.\r\n\r\nTwitter/X: Getting the Twitter API requires 
purchasing X Premium, which is not worth the cost right now. Skip it for now and use the free API providers 
instead.\r\n\r\nThe trigger point is the moderation approval â€” not the article submission. The flow is: article 
submitted â†’ goes to admin panel â†’ moderated â†’ if accepted, Cloudflare Worker fires and distributes it 
everywhere. That is the correct sequence and it is why thinking through all of this in advance is so 
valuable.\r\n\r\n7. Magazine Credibility Strategy\r\nYou might think: if the magazine is open to every single person, 
it will not have any value or credibility. Here is the strategy:\r\n\r\nRight now, open it to everyone.\r\nDo not 
market it as an open platform. Never say \"anyone can publish here.\"\r\nOnce the magazine gets a significant amount 
of traffic, start restricting new signups â€” minimize or reduce the intake of new publishers at that point.\r\nThis 
is how we build credibility over time without sacrificing early growth.\r\n\r\n8. Advertisement System\r\nThe 
advertisement system is for two separate entities: for-profit entities and nonprofit entities. Nonprofit entities will 
be the majority, but we will also be able to earn from for-profit advertisements. I will go into the full details of 
how the advertisement system works in a separate blog post â€” please read that one too, because it contains a 
significant amount of relevant information.\r\n\r\nFor moderation purposes, the advertisement section in 
admin.openrockets.com handles all of this. We do not need to think about whether an advertisement is from a for-profit 
or nonprofit entity when moderating. The only thing we need to check is the same as everything else: is it sensitive, 
personally identifiable, explicit, offensive, a copyright violation, and so on. Same process, same portal.\r\n\r\n9. 
Future Plans (Not Now â€” But Worth Noting)\r\nInstagram API integration â€” When someone publishes a magazine 
article, it could also be automatically posted to their connected Instagram account. Getting the Instagram API is a 
complicated process, so this is a future addition.\r\nMore social platform integrations â€” Same idea, expanded to 
other platforms.\r\n10. Final Note\r\nThe magazine is an extremely important credibility factor for Open Rockets. We 
have no future if we do not get into national newspapers. Every single thing described in this document is possible to 
implement â€” and it is the greatest product we could build. I have spent hours thinking through these ideas and all 
of them can be done.\r\n\r\nIf any changes are needed to the Open Rockets Magazine logo, never hesitate â€” make those 
changes. Just make sure they are reflected everywhere across all sites we 
have.\r\n\r\nShare\r\nShare\r\nComments\r\n\r\nPopular posts from this blog\r\nStarting a High School Nonprofit? Read
